# Introduction To Ai Native Vector Databases

> The primary purpose of vector databases is to provide fast and accurate similarity search or nearest neighbor search capabilities. The integration of AI techniques in vector databases enhances their capabilities, improves search accuracy, optimizes performance, and enables more intelligent and efficient management of high-dimensional data. In this course, Zain Hasan introduces this foundational te

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=0)** What types of data files do you have on your computer, phone, or tablet?
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=5)** Family photos.
>

>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=9)** Emails.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=10)** Call recordings.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=11)** Music videos.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=12)** Let's think about what it means to search and find specific files on our devices.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=18)** What if I ask you to search for a photo of your dog, or the last hip-hop song you listen to, or if I played a tune and asked you to pull up the most similar sounding song to that tune?
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=29)** What if I showed you an image and asked you to pull up a video talking about similar concepts?
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=35)** Searching over files and finding similar ones is one of the most common things we do on our devices, and vector databases enable this critical functionality.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=45)** The superpower of vector databases is that they understand your data using artificial intelligence, and let you search over billions of files.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=53)** They can tell you exactly how similar two files are, and can perform thousands of searches in the blink of an eye.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=60)** Let's look at some real world applications enabled by vector databases.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=65)** You can search for text documents using a text query known as text-to-text search.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=71)** For example, let's say you search for email from your boss.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=75)** They can retrieve the correct email.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=78)** You can search for images using a text query known as text-to-image search.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=83)** For example, let's say you search for photos of my dog and this image might come up.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=88)** Or imagine you want to search for an image using an audio file known as audio-to-image search.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=95)** For example, you could search for the sound of a lion roaring and get back images that match.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=100)** The possibilities are endless.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=103)** Think of what you could build with this type of search functionality at your fingertips.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=107)** Vector databases understand all of these forms of data, and allow you to search over billions of these files to find the most relevant ones in real-time.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=117)** Want to find out how they do it?
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/the-superpower-of-vector-databases?u=76281980&t=119)** Well, come along and join me in this course as we dive into how vector databases enable all of these applications and more.

### 1. Data: What Data Do Vector Databases Store and How Is It Stored?

#### Structured versus unstructured data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=0)** Is an image of a dog more similar to that of a cat or a wolf?
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=5)** Probably the wolf.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=6)** Right.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=7)** Can you give me a number of how similar?
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=11)** This one's a bit more difficult. To put a number to it and quantify how similar two images are is quite difficult.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=18)** Let's explore this further.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=19)** For numerical and structured data, for example, data that can be stored in an Excel file, this type of comparison is quite easy.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=27)** A question could be, which customer is older, earns more, and by how much?
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=32)** We could simply subtract rows and it's easier for us to compare and measure structured data.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=37)** The question then is, how do we compare similarity for unstructured data such as images, audio, video, or even text?
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=45)** Qualitatively comparing these data types is not that hard, but putting an exact number to it is.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=51)** This is because structured data is easy to compare numerically, and we can use computers to perform mathematical operations on it.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=59)** Computers understand and talk numbers, but unstructured data is hard to understand and quantify as numbers.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=66)** Ignoring this difficulty for now, let's assume we have a way to translate any unstructured data.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=72)** So think processing images, sentences, or audio files into a set of numbers.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=78)** Then, we could use computers and mathematical operations to answer how similar an image of a dog is to that of a cat or a wolf.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=86)** We could just compare their respective numerical representations, and we'd be done.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=90)** What would be a good way to capture the meaning of an unstructured data point as a number or even a set of numbers?
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=97)** Perhaps one of these numbers could be how many different colors are used in an image?
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=102)** Another could be how many objects are there in the image?
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=106)** Are the colors blurred?
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=108)** Are the objects upright?
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=110)** We can answer lots of similar questions about these visual features to capture the meaning behind an image as a combination of numbers, bringing it one step closer to a structured format.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=121)** This is similar to what a vector database does.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=124)** It translates unstructured data into numbers that can be understood in this manner, and then uses these numbers to compare between data points.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=133)** Some important questions to think about are how do computers translate unstructured data?
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=139)** And how do we make sure meaning doesn't get lost in this translation from human to machine language?
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=145)** Here, we talked about the main difference between structured and unstructured data, and how it can be difficult for computers to understand the meaning of unstructured data points like images, sentences, and audio files.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/structured-versus-unstructured-data?u=76281980&t=158)** In the next video, we'll discuss more differences between human-understandable data and machin-understandable data to get a better idea of how we can translate from one to the other.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **CLI Commands:** cat (2), make (1)

> **Cross-References:** we talked about (1), in the next (1)
> **Definitions:** is an  (1)

#### Human-understandable versus machine-understandable data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=0)** In the previous video, we introduced some differences between structured and unstructured data.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=5)** Now let's think about how all of these unstructured data types we've discussed previously, text, images, audio, and video can be understood by humans.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=15)** Words in a text document can be read and understood one after the other.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=20)** Images stored as pixels of colors stacked on top of each other are understood as objects interacting with each other.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=27)** Audio files, stored as sound intensities and frequencies can be listened to and understood as music or audiobooks, and et cetera.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=36)** Videos are time evolutions of lots and lots of images that can be understood when played forward in time.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=43)** This is how we consume media stored in data files through our eyes, ears, and might.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=48)** How do computers understand the same data?
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=51)** Let's examine one by one.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=54)** An image can be thought of as millions of pixels stacked on top of each other, each having a unique number value contributing slightly to the overall picture.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=65)** We can string these pixels out and understand these as a trail of numbers, one beside the other.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=71)** We can then perform mathematical operations on these pixel values to capture visual features of what's in the image itself.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=79)** These visual features are themselves numbers that can be stored in a computer, and used to understand and summarize what's in the image.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=88)** A text document can be turned into pieces of words, each of which gets a numerical ID.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=94)** We can then capture the meaning of words by looking at what other words it's used with.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=100)** This is known as the co-occurrence of words and can be used to understand their meanings.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=105)** For example, if I say three sentences, the tiger played with her cubs versus the jaguar played with her cubs versus he put the lettuce into the salad. Here due to similar words co-occurring, you can understand that a tiger and jaguar are more similar to each other than to lettuce.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=129)** We can do the same for audio and video files, each of which can be represented as numbers for the computer to understand, and then these numbers can be summarized into smaller groups of numbers, having to do with the actual content, patterns and concepts found in the data.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=146)** Computers understand all data as these groups of numbers, and these groups of numbers are known as vectors.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=154)** Vectors capture the meaning behind the data, allowing a computer to understand data similar to how we do.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=161)** For example, to a human, you would say, pass the salt, please.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=167)** But to a computer, you'd have to say pass the 1.2, 1.1, 6.2, 1.3, 9.8, please.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=174)** That set of numbers is what a computer knows salt as. They understand this vector representation better.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=182)** Similar to when translating from one language to another, we want to make sure our data makes sense to computers as vectors, and we want to ensure that, if possible, no meaning is lost in translation when going from human understandable data representations to machine understandable data representations or vectors.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=201)** So, to summarize, vector databases use vector data representations to understand data.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=206)** That's why they're called vector databases.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=209)** In the next video, we'll talk about what these vectors actually look like.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/huma-understandable-versus-machine-understandable-data?u=76281980&t=213)** See you there.

#### Introduce the concept of distance between two vectors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=0)** Now that we understand that unstructured data can be represented as vectors, let's think about similar and dissimilar data points in vector space.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=9)** Let's go back to our example of the vector representations of a dog, cat, and wolf.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=13)** These vectors are very high dimensional, but if we plot them out on our grid, we would likely see the wolf and dog are similar, whereas the cat is different.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=23)** Let's calculate how far apart these vectors are.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=26)** There are multiple ways to measure the distance between two vectors.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=30)** We could use Euclidean distance L2.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=33)** This is the shortest distance between two vectors, and can be visualized as the length of the line that connects the head of the two vectors you're comparing.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=42)** You could also use Manhattan distance L1.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=45)** This is the distance measure from one vector to the other if you could only move along the dimensions of your data.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=53)** Another popular distance metric we could use is called the cosine distance.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=58)** This is a measure of how similar the directions of the vectors are, and doesn't care about the length of the vectors.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=64)** We could also use the dot product to measure how different two vectors are.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=69)** This is the length of one vector measured in the direction of the other vector.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=74)** You can think of it as the length of the projection of one vector on the other.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=79)** We can use any one of these metrics to calculate how different or similar to vector representations of data points are from each other.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=87)** If we measure how far away the dog vector is from the wolf vector, we can quantify how similar a dog is to a wolf.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=95)** I can give you a number using any one of these metrics.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=99)** This is how we can quantify how similar two data points are to each other.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=104)** Now, think of any data point.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=106)** If you can represent it as a vector, then you can automatically measure how similar it is to your other vectorized data points.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=114)** This is how vector databases measure the difference between any two data points.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=118)** And you can do this with any data you can represent as vectors.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=122)** That's pretty powerful.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=124)** This same approach works for images, text, videos, audio, and et cetera.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/introduce-the-concept-of-distance-between-two-vectors?u=76281980&t=130)** In the next video, we'll put what we've learned so far to the test with a challenge involving creating and visualizing vectors and measuring the distances between them.

>

#### Solution: Working with vectors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=5)** Now that you've taken the time to work on this challenge, let me demonstrate my solution.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=11)** So, in my solution, let's start talking about the question itself, where we've been given two vectors that represent RGB colors.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=18)** So, the color 40, 120, and 60, and then the color is 60, 50, and 90.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=24)** Now again, remember these numbers have to do with how much red you need for this color, how much green you need, and then how much blue you need?
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=32)** So, these are RGB colors.
>

>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=320)** The first thing that we're going to do is calculate the Manhattan distance between these two vectors.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=325)** So, to calculate Manhattan distance, we're essentially starting off at one vector, and we're walking along these axes, one by one, and we're adding up how much distance we've covered until we get to the other vector.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=339)** So, to mathematically do that, we're going to go in, and we're going to start off and we're going to say, what is the distance between the individual color coordinates?
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=350)** so, we're going to say color one and color two, and we're going to walk from one to the other.
>
> **[5:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=355)** We're going to take the difference between all of these for each one of the dimensions.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=359)** So, we're going to loop over and we're going to start off go from zero, one and two to access the R, the G, and the B.
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=369)** So, this code is going to extract for us and give us the difference between the red intensities, the green intensities, and the blue intensities for our colors.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=379)** So, once we run this, we can see that the differences are here.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=384)** And this is still not the Manhattan distance so, we still need to modify this.
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=388)** We're going to take this, and we're going to say that we want to take this output, and we're going to run that through the absolute value function, so we're going to pass this in here, and we're going to sum all of this up.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=400)** And this is the exact same formula that we saw earlier for the Manhattan distance.
>
> **[6:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=405)** So, now, when we run this, we get the correct Manhattan distance.
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=409)** So, the color one is 120 units away from the color two based on the Manhattan distance measuring from one vector to the other vector.
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=420)** An alternate approach to calculate the exact same distance is just to use the built-in library in NumPy, the linear algebra library here, to calculate the norm between a and b of order one.
>
> **[7:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=432)** This is why Manhattan distance is also known as the L1 distance.
>
> **[7:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=435)** So, we can run this, and we can verify that we get the exact same distance here as well.
>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=440)** Next, we're going to look at Euclidean distance.
>
> **[7:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=443)** Euclidean distance if we scroll up, here is the shortest distance between this vector and this vector.
>
> **[7:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=450)** So, it's literally the line that connects the orange dot to the blue dot.
>
> **[7:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=455)** If we want to know how long that line is, the Euclidean distance is going to give us that measurement.
>
> **[7:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=460)** So, the way to do this is we're going to set it up, and similarly, we're going to go through and we're going to extract out how different each color is.
>
> **[7:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=470)** So, we're going to go to the first dimension of color one, we're going to take the first dimension of color two, and we're going to loop through this and we're going to square them.
>
> **[8:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=480)** So, we're going to exponentiate them to the power of two.
>
> **[8:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=482)** And we're going to loop through for all of our intensity.
>
> **[8:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=486)** So, the R, the G, and the B intensity.
>
> **[8:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=489)** Range length, and we want to make sure that we get to all the dimensions.
>
> **[8:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=493)** So, we specify that over here.
>
> **[8:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=496)** So, now we can plot this out.
>
> **[8:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=498)** And this is the square of the distances that we have between them.

>

> **[8:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=509)** So, it's a bit of a sanity check here.
>
> **[8:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=511)** So, we're not done.
>
> **[8:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=513)** So, the next thing that we need to do is take these and we need to pass it into the remaining formula that we showed earlier.
>
> **[8:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=519)** So, we're going to take the square root of this information.
>
> **[8:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=524)** I'm going to pass in the NumPy array which goes in and it takes this Python list, turns it into a NumPy array, and it sums across the dimensions, and it takes a square root.
>
> **[8:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=536)** So, now we get our Euclidean distance between these two vectors.
>
> **[9:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=541)** One small thing to note here.
>
> **[9:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=543)** The direct line between the orange and the blue data point is smaller than walking along these axes to get from the orange to the blue, or vice versa.
>
> **[9:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=553)** And as a consequence of that, the Manhattan distance is larger than the Euclidean distance.
>
> **[9:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=559)** The Euclidean distance is only 78.74 around that, and the Manhattan distance is 120 units.
>
> **[9:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=565)** Another way to do this, an alternate way is to use the built-in NumPy library linear algebra, where we can go in and ask for the norm between a and b, and ask for a order two norm.
>
> **[9:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=577)** That's why Euclidean distance is also known as the L2 norm.
>
> **[9:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=580)** So, we can do that and verify that we get the exact same answer.
>
> **[9:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=584)** So, the next distance we're going to cover is the cosine distance.
>
> **[9:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=588)** And the cosine distance measures the difference in directionality between the two vectors.
>
> **[9:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=594)** So, for example, if this vector was pushed slightly over here, the cosine distance would increase because the direction would be more different.
>
> **[10:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=603)** So, how do we measure cosine distance?
>
> **[10:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=605)** We can go in here.
>
> **[10:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=606)** And the cosine distance as we talked about earlier has to do with directionality.
>
> **[10:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=612)** And it has nothing to do with the actual length of the vectors.
>
> **[10:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=617)** So, we can start off by calculating the dot product between the two vectors.
>
> **[10:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=622)** So, we can take the dot product between these two vectors, and we normalize that with the length of the individual vectors.
>
> **[10:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=629)** We can calculate the length of the individual vectors using the linear algebra library that we've been using.
>
> **[10:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=634)** And here, we can ask for the norm of the first vector multiplied by the norm of the second vector.
>
> **[10:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=644)** Norm of the second vector can run that.
>
> **[10:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=648)** And that gives us the cosine distance.
>
> **[10:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=650)** So, the cosine distance at maximum is 1.0.
>
> **[10:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=654)** And this is a very high cosine distance.
>
> **[10:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=656)** And what this tells us is that these two vectors have pretty much the same directionality, which is apparent from the visual as well.
>
> **[11:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=664)** They're pointing in the same direction, the cosine distance is high.
>
> **[11:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=667)** If they were pointing in different directions, the cosine distance would be low.
>
> **[11:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=671)** The cosine distance goes from -1 to 1.
>
> **[11:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=674)** If the two vectors are at 90 degrees to each other, the cosine distance is zero.
>
> **[11:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=679)** If the vectors are exact opposite, they're pointing in the exact opposite directions, then, the cosine distance is negative one.
>
> **[11:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=685)** So, the last thing that we're going to do is talk about the dot product distance between these two vectors. In order to calculate the dot product between the two, we can just go in and multiply the individual dimensions together.
>
> **[11:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=697)** So, we're going to take the first dimension or the i'th dimension of color one and the i'th dimension of color two, where I loops from the beginning to the last dimension for i in range length.
>
> **[11:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=713)** And so, we're going to loop through this.
>
> **[11:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=716)** Look at what the multiplication is.
>
> **[11:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=719)** We're going to take this, and we're going to turn this into a NumPy array.
>
> **[12:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=723)** So, we're going to pass this into the NumPy array function, and we're going to take that and we're going to add them all up.
>
> **[12:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=731)** So, we're going to sum this.
>
> **[12:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=733)** And that gives us the dot product between the two vectors.
>
> **[12:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=736)** Another easy way to do this is just to say NumPy and use the dot product functionality built into NumPy.
>
> **[12:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=743)** So, we can say np dot between a and b, and we get the exact same result.
>
> **[12:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=749)** For the last question, we want to come up with a color that has a similar cosine distance to the first vector or the first color that we're given.
>
> **[12:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=758)** So, in this case, the first color is 40, 120, and 60.
>
> **[12:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=763)** We want to come up with a color or a color three here that is in the same direction or as close to the first color as possible.
>
> **[12:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=773)** So, to do this, what we're going to do is simply go in and start off at color one, the red intensity.
>
> **[13:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=781)** We're going to slightly nudge it. If we want to say how much red should it take to get to color three from color one?
>
> **[13:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=789)** We're just going to nudge it a little bit, and we're going to say, okay, maybe we'll add a little bit more red.
>
> **[13:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-working-with-vectors?u=76281980&t=793)** We don't want to modify it too much, so we'll just modify it a little bit.

### 2. Natural Querying: How Do You Search for Data in a Vector Database?

#### Frame the query as a question or search
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=0)** Now that we understand, vectors can be used to capture the meaning behind data, and can also be understood by machines, let's imagine all of our data in vector form.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=10)** We can take all the data files that we have on our computer, or information that our company wants to store and represent them as vectors.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=17)** This is known as embedding your data into vector space.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=20)** We visualized each of these vectors as a green point in this visual.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=25)** You can embed text files, images, audio, video, and more into vector space.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=31)** In this vector space, similar file vectors are closer together, while vectors for dissimilar files are further apart.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=39)** Now, let's think about how we can search through this data.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=43)** Searching involves asking a question or querying.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=47)** For example, you might want to retrieve the images of your family vacation from your computer.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=53)** We now want to take the question and see which information on our computer is relevant.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=58)** How can we search through the tens of thousands of data points and retrieve the ones that are relevant to the question or query?
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=64)** There are many algorithms that can be used to quantify how relevant a data point is to our question or query.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=71)** But to keep it simple, the search process is essentially the same as measuring the distance between our question vector and every other file vector representation in our computer.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=82)** This is known as vector search or semantic search.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=85)** In our green vector space, imagine the query as a red vector.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=89)** All the green points closest to the red query vector represent data objects similar in meaning to our query.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=96)** At the heart of it, this is the essence of vector search.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=101)** In this video, we explain vector search, framing the search process as a questioning or querying process that retrieves and returns relevant data points.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/frame-the-query-as-a-question-or-search?u=76281980&t=110)** In the next few videos, we'll see how you can actually perform vector search using a vector database.

#### Generate the question in machine-understandable language
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980&t=0)** In the last video, we framed searching over objects as asking a question or query and then retrieving the answers most similar to the query, in order to measure how close the query is to the objects in our database, we need to use vectors.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980&t=15)** Since every data object in our vector database is represented as a vector, when comparing them to a question or query, we want to think of it as a vector as well.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980&t=25)** We can take the question and frame this as a vector previously seen as the red vector.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980&t=31)** We now want to take the question and see which information on our computer is relevant.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980&t=36)** We can measure how close the question vector is to each object vector and get a distance between each.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980&t=42)** This gives us a vector similarity between the question and the data.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980&t=47)** We can take each distance and then order them from lowest distance to highest distance.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980&t=52)** This allows you to sort your data from most similar to least similar to the query vector.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980&t=58)** And remember that each vector represents a data point in the real world, so you can return those real objects to the user as answers to their query.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980&t=66)** We've now fully covered how to perform vector or semantic searches using a query and object stored in a vector database.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/generate-the-question-in-machine-understandable-language?u=76281980&t=74)** In the next video, we'll see how you can set up and perform vector search using Python and the open-source vector database Weaviate.

#### Adding data to a vector database

> **[10:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-vector-search-with-weaviate?u=76281980&t=625)** How do we know that they're double jeopardy round questions?
>
> **[10:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-vector-search-with-weaviate?u=76281980&t=628)** We can go up here, and we can concatenate this, and we can say, okay, I also want to know the round information for this.
>
> **[10:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-vector-search-with-weaviate?u=76281980&t=634)** So, if I re-run this query now, we get the exact same results.
>
> **[10:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-vector-search-with-weaviate?u=76281980&t=638)** But now the round is double jeopardy.
>
> **[10:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-vector-search-with-weaviate?u=76281980&t=641)** And with that, we've successfully answered the fourth question.
>
> **[10:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-vector-search-with-weaviate?u=76281980&t=645)** This chapter introduced the concept of vector search and how you can use Weaviate, an open-source vector database to start performing vector search over your own data.
>
> **[10:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-vector-search-with-weaviate?u=76281980&t=653)** In the next chapter, we'll see how machine learning models actually generate vectors and how we can practically specify these models for use in Weaviate.
>
> **[11:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/solution-vector-search-with-weaviate?u=76281980&t=661)** We'll even build an image and text search engine.

### 3. Machine Learning Vectors: How Does a Vector Database Understand Your Data?

#### Machine learning models and object classification
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=0)** We spoke earlier about translating from human-understandable data to machine-understandable data, or vectors, as we later called them.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=7)** In this chapter, we'll explore how this translation happens.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=11)** Vector databases use machine learning models to translate data into vector representations, such that as little meaning is lost in translation as possible.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=21)** Most often, these models are neural networks.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=23)** Machine-learning models can translate similar objects to vectors that are closer together in vector space, and dissimilar objects to vectors that are farther apart, thus retaining a lot of the meaning behind the vectors.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=36)** How do machine learning models know where to locate each data point in vector space?
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=41)** This is best understood using an analogy. In a library books are placed in the correct location using the Dewey Decimal System. Depending on its topic, each book has an appropriate location in the library.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=53)** Books on Latin grammar, for example, start with location number 475, while books about sanitary engineering start at location 628.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=62)** When a librarian is asked to retrieve a book about a specific topic, they know approximately where in the library to start looking for it using this system.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=71)** Similarly, machine learning models are trained to identify concepts and data objects and locate them in vector space accordingly by translating them into vectors.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=81)** Good machine learning models will retain a lot of the meaning in the data objects when converting them to vector form.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=87)** And it's for this very reason that machine learning models are critical to understanding how vector databases work.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=94)** The reason why we Weaviate is known as an AI native vector database is because it relies on and uses AI models to generate the vectors, which are then used to perform vector search.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=106)** Using AI models, we understand how data objects according to their concepts can be located in vector space.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/machine-learning-models-and-object-classification?u=76281980&t=113)** In the next video, we'll look deeper into these models to learn how they understand data and capture this meaning in vector form.

#### Translating data from human to machine-understandable

>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/translating-data-from-human-to-machine-understandable?u=76281980&t=98)** Vectors.

### 4. Scalability: What Does a Vector Database Need to Do?

#### Scalability: When to use a vector DB
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=0)** In the last chapter, we learned about how vector databases use machine learning models to generate vectors for data points.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=7)** We even built our own multimedia search functionality that allows us to search with images and text.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=13)** In this chapter, we'll discuss scaling up with vector databases, how to assess how good a vector database is, and how you can perform CRUD operations with vector databases.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=23)** Let's start off with scalability.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=25)** If you have tens of millions or even billions of data objects and want to perform vector search or semantic search over them, this is where other solutions will slow down and you'll need vector databases.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=36)** They're optimized to perform mathematical operations on vectors and can retrieve relevant objects in real-time, even when you have billions of vectors in the database.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=46)** Vector databases use a class of algorithms called approximate nearest neighbors, ANN, to find which vectors are in the nearest neighborhood of the query vector.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=56)** This method allows them to efficiently search over vectors without having to compare the query vector to every stored vector, one at a time, like we demonstrated in an earlier video.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=66)** The downside here is that we might not always find the closest nearest neighbors.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=70)** There is a possibility that we skip some of these data points.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=73)** This is why this method is called approximate nearest neighbors.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=77)** The trade-off were explicitly making when using a vector database, and ANNs is that we're giving up on accuracy or recall for faster performance.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=87)** The most popular used ANN algorithm for vector databases is called Hierarchical Navigable Small Worlds, HNSW.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=95)** This algorithm builds a graph out of the vectors and sets up a hierarchy of points, with further away points in the upper level, and closer together points in the lower levels.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=107)** When searching over this layered graph, we start off at the top of the graph, and we travel across objects that are far apart to find the one closest to our query.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=117)** When we've gotten as close as possible to the query, we then drop down one level and try to get closer to the query at this lower level that has more vectors.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=126)** This keeps on repeating until we find the k closest neighbors at the lowest level and return them.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=133)** This algorithm makes it so that you don't have to compare and search every vector to the query vector.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=139)** The long-range comparisons made earlier on are very efficient at identifying which smaller subset of vectors should be searched over in the lower levels.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=149)** The ANN algorithm allows a vector database to scale up to billions of objects and still perform real-time searches.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/scalability-when-to-use-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=157)** In the next video, we'll look at which metrics we can use to assess the performance of a vector database.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** ann (3), crud (1), hnsw (1)
> **CLI Commands:** find (4)
> **Cross-References:** in the last (1), in the next (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1), is called (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (1)
> **Prerequisites:** you'll need (1)

#### Ways to measure performance of a vector DB
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=0)** In the last video, we spoke about the HNSW ANN algorithm, which is the core technology that allows vector databases to perform real-time searches over lots and lots of data.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=10)** In this video, we'll introduce some concepts that will help you assess the performance of a vector database.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=16)** Depending on your use case, some of these metrics might be more important than others.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=20)** Going back to using ANN, this means that not all nearest neighbors might be returned.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=26)** In order to measure how reliably and accurately nearest neighbors are returned, we use a metric called recall.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=33)** Recall is defined as the number of retrieved nearest neighbors by ANN over the number of total nearest neighbors if there were no approximations.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=43)** The higher the recall, the better.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=45)** It means that our database is accurately retrieving similar objects to the query, and not missing any of the neighbors that should be returned.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=53)** If we want to perform real-time search with our vector database, we'll have to compromise on recall to get more performance.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=60)** We measure performance using latency or queries per second.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=64)** QPS, as the name implies, is a measure of speed and tells you the number of queries you can successfully run per second to get relevant objects from the database.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=73)** The higher the QPS, the better.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=76)** Usually, the trade-off is between QPSs and recall.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=80)** The higher the QPS you need, the lower the recall you'll have to suffer, as searching faster over the vector index might mean missing some of the neighboring vectors.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=89)** Another very useful metric is how much memory the vector database takes up.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=93)** In fact, for individual users, this might be the most important metric because vector databases perform search in memory using RAM and thus require lots of it, which is not good since RAM is very expensive and thus might be the limiting factor.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=107)** If you can reduce the memory usage of a vector database and still keep the same performance and recall, then this is ideal.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=114)** Some vector databases, such as Weaviate, use vector compression algorithms that save you space and still offer good recall and queries per second.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=123)** So to conclude, we introduce three metrics recall, QPS, and memory usage.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=129)** These measure how correct the database is or how reliably it can return the correct nearest neighbors, how fast it is, and how much memory it costs to run it.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/introduction-to-ai-native-vector-databases/ways-to-measure-performance-of-a-vector-db?u=76281980&t=138)** In the next video, we'll learn about another characteristic feature of vector databases, namely support for CRUD operations.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** qps (4), ann (3), ram (2), hnsw (1), crud (1)
> **Definitions:** means that (2), defined as (1), is a  (1)
> **Code Keywords:** case, (1), require (1)
> **Cross-References:** in the last (1), in the next (1)
> **Analogies:** such as (1)
